Preferring Possessor's Evidence in Unique Ownership Disputes
Ruling with proof on preferring possessor's evidence for non-repetitive causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the possessor's evidence is preferred when both parties present evidence of a non-repetitive ownership cause, and explain the proof from the hadith of the she-camel.

Core Idea
The possessor's evidence is preferred when the basis of ownership is unique and non-repetitive.

How does a judge decide when both the claimant and the possessor bring witnesses claiming ownership based on the same event, such as the birth of an animal? This lesson presents a specific Hanafi rule derived from a hadith: when the cause of ownership is unique and cannot be repeated, the evidence of the current possessor is given precedence over the non-possessor's evidence. We will examine the rule, its proof, and its application to analogous cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Recognize that the dispute involves a unique, non-repetitive cause of ownership (e.g., birth of an animal, weaving of a one-time garment). Both parties, one in possession and one not, claim ownership based on that same event and produce evidence.
The cause must be such that it can logically occur only once regarding the item (e.g., a specific animal's birth).
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi school rules that in such a case, the evidence of the possessor (sahib al-yad) is given priority over the evidence of the outsider (al-kharaj). The possessor is more entitled to the object.
This is an exception to the general rule that the defendant (possessor) normally only takes an oath.
3
Analyze the dalil (evidence)
The hadith from Jabir narrates that two men disputed over a she-camel, each claiming it was born in his possession with evidence. The Prophet (SAW) adjudicated in favor of the one in whose hand it was. This hadith directly supports the ruling.
The hadith is ahad but accepted in practice.
4
Explain the wajh al-dalalah
The legal implication (wajh al-dalalah) is that the Prophet's judgment for the possessor, despite both having evidence, indicates that the possessor's evidence is stronger when the claim involves a unique cause like birth. The uniqueness of the cause strengthens the possessor's claim.
The hadith is considered as evidence (dalil) for preferring the possessor.
5
Apply the analogy (qiyas)
The rule extends by analogy to any cause of ownership that does not recur (e.g., weaving of a cloth that is woven only once, or creating a unique item). The common ‘illah is the non-repetitive nature, which makes the possessor's evidence a stronger indicator of true ownership.
If the cause is repeatable (e.g., purchase), this rule does not apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان اقام الخارج وصاحب اليد كل واحد منهما بينة بالنتاج فصاحب اليد اولى و كذلک النسج في الثياب التي لا تنسج الا مرة واحدة وكذلك كل سبب فى الملك لا يتكرر. عن جابر ان رجلين اختصما إلى النبي الله في ناقة فقال كل واحد منهما نتجت هذه الناقة عندى واقام بينة فقضى بها رسول الله للذي هي في يديه (رواه البيهقي)
If the outsider (non-possessor) and the possessor each produce evidence of birth (al-nataj), then the possessor is more entitled. Similarly, regarding weaving (al-nasj) in garments that are only woven once, and likewise every cause in ownership that does not recur. From Jabir: Two men disputed before the Prophet (SAW) over a she-camel; each said, 'This camel was born in my possession' and produced evidence. The Messenger of Allah (SAW) adjudicated it for the one in whose hands it was.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the chapter of claims (da'wa). The issue: when both the claimant (kharaj, the one not in possession) and the respondent (sahib al-yad, the possessor) present evidence that the property (e.g., a slave or animal) was born in their possession, the evidence of the possessor is preferred. This is despite the general principle that the defendant's evidence is not normally accepted because he is under oath. However, here an additional indicator (sign) of ownership—the unique, non-repetitive nature of birth—tips the scale. The same rule applies to weaving of cloth that is only woven once, and any other cause of ownership that cannot be repeated, such as the creation of a unique item. The hadith of Jabir confirms: when two claimants each claimed a she-camel was born in their custody, the Prophet (SAW) awarded it to the possessor. This ruling is qiyas (analogy) based on the hadith. The commentary emphasizes that the possessor's evidence is accepted due to the strength of the sign, even though he is the defendant.

Key Terms
النتاج (al-nataj): Birth of an animal or slave; a cause of ownership that occurs only once.
النسج (al-nasj): Weaving of cloth, which is a one-time act of production.
صاحب اليد (sahib al-yad): The person who currently has physical possession of the disputed property.
الخارج (al-kharaj): The claimant who does not have possession; the outsider.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute where both the possessor and non-possessor claim ownership based on birth, whose evidence is preferred according to the Hanafi school?
A) The non-possessor's evidence
B) The possessor's evidence
C) Both are equal
D) No evidence is accepted

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when both parties produce evidence of a non-repetitive ownership cause (birth, weaving, etc.), the possessor's evidence is given precedence, based on the hadith of the she-camel.

Preference of Possessor's Evidence of Purchase over Claimant's Absolute Ownership
Ruling preferring possessor's specific purchase evidence over absolute ownership claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that specific ownership evidence (purchase) outweighs absolute ownership evidence in property disputes and explain the underlying reasoning.

Core Idea
Specific ownership (mulk muqayyad) outweighs absolute ownership (mulk mutlaq) in evidence.

In property disputes, a claimant may prove he once owned the property (absolute ownership), while the possessor proves he purchased it from that very claimant. The Hanafi school gives precedence to the possessor's evidence because it is more specific and indicates a valid transfer. This lesson examines the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Understand the two parties: a claimant (kharaj) who proves absolute ownership without specifying a cause, and a possessor (dhu al-yad) who proves a specific purchase from that claimant.
The purchase must be from the same claimant for the rule to apply.
2
Distinguish between absolute and specific ownership
Absolute ownership (mulk mutlaq) is ownership without a cause; specific ownership (mulk muqayyad) is ownership with a specified cause like purchase.
Specific ownership is considered stronger evidence.
3
State the Hanafi preference
The possessor's evidence of specific purchase is preferred over the claimant's evidence of absolute ownership.
This is a tarjih (preference) rule in evidence law.
4
Understand the proof
The ruling is based on a hadith where a specific cause (birth) was preferred over a mere claim, indicating that specific evidence is stronger.
The hadith is narrated by Jabir.
5
Apply the rule to similar cases
The rule applies whenever one party proves absolute ownership and the other proves a specific transfer from that party.
If both have specific evidence, the possessor generally prevails due to possession (yad).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان اقام الخارج بينة على الملك المطلق وصاحب اليد بينة على الشراء منه كان صاحب اليد اولى
If the outsider presents evidence of absolute ownership and the possessor presents evidence of purchase from him, then the possessor is more entitled.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This ruling concerns a situation where the claimant (kharaj) proves that he owned the property at some point (absolute ownership, without specifying how), while the possessor proves he purchased it from that very claimant. The Hanafi school prefers the possessor's evidence because it is more specific (mulk muqayyad) and indicates a transfer of ownership. The hadith of the she-camel is also cited as proof, where the specific claim of birth (a specific cause) was preferred. The possessor's evidence of purchase provides a clear narrative of how ownership passed from the claimant to him, making his case stronger.

Key Terms
الملك المطلق (al-mulk al-mutlaq): Absolute ownership without specifying its cause (e.g., purchase, gift, inheritance).
الملك المقيد (al-mulk al-muqayyad): Ownership with a specified cause, such as purchase or gift.
الشراء (al-shira'): Purchase contract; a specific cause of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a property dispute, the claimant proves absolute ownership, and the possessor proves he purchased it from the claimant. Who is preferred?
A) The claimant
B) The possessor
C) Both are equal
D) The property is divided

Key Takeaway
Specific ownership (mulk muqayyad) evidenced by purchase takes precedence over absolute ownership (mulk mutlaq) evidence.

Conflicting Evidence Without Dates: Abu Hanifa vs. Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on evidence cancellation when dates are absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position when two litigants present conflicting evidence of purchase without dates, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
When both parties present equal evidence without dates, both evidences are dropped—but Imam Muhammad argues for the plaintiff's evidence.

This passage deals with a scenario where two individuals each claim to have purchased a property from a third party, but neither can provide a date. The main Hanafi view is that both evidences cancel out, leaving the property with the current possessor. However, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani offers a different reasoning, prioritizing the plaintiff's claim. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your grasp of Hanafi evidence hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the main position
Identify the factual setting: two people claim to have bought the same property from a third party, but neither has a date. The main Hanafi ruling is that both evidences are dropped (suqut al-bayyinatan).
The possessor is the defendant; the other is the plaintiff.
2
Identify the basis for dropping both evidences
The evidences are equal in strength and cannot be reconciled, so they cancel each other out, leaving the status quo (possession) intact.
This is the default view attributed to Abu Hanifa.
3
Present Imam Muhammad's counter-argument
Imam Muhammad argues that the plaintiff's evidence should be accepted because the plaintiff is the mudda'i (claimant) and the possessor is the mudda'a alayh (defendant). He assumes a chronological order where the plaintiff ultimately acquired ownership.
This is a minority view within the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the main view, the property stays with the possessor; under Muhammad's view, it goes to the plaintiff.
This affects who bears the burden of proof and the outcome of the case.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that both evidences are dropped (the view of Abu Hanifa). Imam Muhammad's view is acknowledged but not preferred for fatwa.
This reflects the school's precedence on the authority of Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان اقام كل واحد منهما البينة على الشراء من اخر ولا تاريخ لهما سقطت البينتان. قال محمد: بل تقبل بينة الخارج لأنه مدعي وصاحب اليد مدعى عليه.
If each one presents evidence of purchase from another and there is no date for either, both evidences are dropped. Muhammad said: Rather, the evidence of the outsider is accepted because he is the plaintiff and the possessor is the defendant.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. The base ruling (attributed to Abu Hanifa or the earlier authorities) states that when both litigants produce evidence that they purchased the property from a third party, but neither provides a date, both evidences are dropped (saqita). Consequently, the property remains with the possessor (since he is the defendant and his evidence is dropped, but the claimant's evidence is also dropped, so the status quo prevails). Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) disagrees: he argues that since the outsider (kharaj) is the plaintiff (mudda'i) and the possessor is the defendant (mudda'a alayh), the plaintiff's evidence should be accepted, and the property awarded to him. He then explains that we assume a chronological order: first the possessor bought from his seller, then the plaintiff's seller bought, then the plaintiff bought, so the plaintiff ultimately acquired ownership. This is a detailed juristic reasoning. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school may follow Abu Hanifa (the main ruling) that both are dropped, but this passage shows the internal ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
سقطة البينتان (suqutat al-bayyinatan): Both evidences are invalidated and not considered.
مدعي (mudda'i): The plaintiff in a legal claim.
مدعى عليه (mudda'a alayh): The defendant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the main Hanafi position, if both litigants present evidence of purchase from a third party but no dates are given, what happens to the evidences?
A) Both are accepted and the property is divided
B) Both are dropped and the property stays with the possessor
C) The claimant's evidence is accepted
D) The possessor's evidence is accepted

Key Takeaway
When conflicting purchase evidence lacks dates, the Hanafi rule drops both, preserving the status quo; Imam Muhammad's view for the plaintiff is not the mufta bihi.

Oath by Divorce or Manumission: Prohibition and Proof
Ruling on prohibited oath formulas in litigation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on making a defendant swear by divorce or manumission, identify the hadith prohibition, and explain the cross-madhhab difference.

Core Idea
Swearing by divorce or manumission in court is prohibited; only oaths by Allah are permissible.

In this lesson from Kitab al-Da'wa, we examine a key procedural ruling: a judge must not administer an oath to a defendant using divorce or manumission formulas. The text grounds this prohibition in a prophetic hadith that restricts lawful oaths to Allah's name alone. We will unpack the ruling, its evidence, and why the Hanafi school differs from the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The matn states: 'ولا يستحلف بالطلاق ولا بالعتاق' – the defendant shall not be made to swear by divorce nor by manumission. The ruling is prohibition (makruh tahrimi).
The commentary explains this as a prohibition based on a hadith.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith cited: one should not swear by other than Allah, or swear by Allah, or remain silent. This is a general prohibition.
The exact Arabic of the hadith is not quoted in the text but is referenced.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Swearing by divorce or manumission is swearing by other than Allah, thus included in the prohibition. The hadith does not exempt these formulas.
Divorce and manumission are created acts, not divine attributes.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school generally permits oaths by divorce as a valid oath form, considering it a serious matter that serves as deterrence.
This difference stems from different interpretations of the hadith and the legal scope of prohibitions.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school adheres to the literal prohibition in the hadith; thus, the mufta bihi position is prohibition.
The judge should use only Allah's name or ask the defendant to remain silent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[2771](42) ولا يستحلف بالطلاق ولا بالعتاق (2771) (42) قسم نہ لی جائے طلاق کی اور آزاد کرنے کی۔ یوں قسم نہ کھلائے کہ ایسا نہیں کیا تو میری بیوی کو طلاق یا میرا غلام آزاد۔ او پر حدیث آئی کہ اللہ کے علاوہ کی قسم نہ کھائے یا اللہ کی قسم کھائے یا چپ رہے۔ اس لئے طلاق اور عتاق کی بھی قسم نہ کھلائے۔
And he (the defendant) shall not be made to swear by divorce nor by manumission. (42) Oath should not be taken by divorce or freeing. Do not make him swear like: 'If not, my wife is divorced or my slave is free.' A hadith came that one should not swear by other than Allah, or swear by Allah, or remain silent. Therefore, oaths by divorce and manumission are also not to be administered.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This is a ruling from Kitab al-Da'wa. The text states that the defendant in a lawsuit should not be made to swear an oath by divorce (talaq) or by manumission (itaq). The reasoning, as explained in the commentary, is based on a hadith that prohibits swearing by anything other than Allah. If one wishes to swear, it should be by Allah or remain silent. Therefore, even though divorce and manumission are serious matters, they should not be used as oath formulas in court. The judge should avoid such phrasing.

Key Terms
يستحلف (yustahlif): The act of a judge making a litigant swear an oath.
طلاق (talaq): Divorce; here used as a conditional oath formula.
عتاق (itāq): Manumission of a slave; used as an oath formula.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on making a defendant swear by divorce or freeing a slave in a Hanafi court?
A) It is recommended.
B) It is permitted but disliked.
C) It is prohibited; the defendant should only swear by Allah.
D) It is required if the plaintiff demands it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi court procedure, only oaths by Allah are permitted; oaths by divorce or manumission are prohibited based on a Prophetic hadith.

Prohibition of Swearing Non-Muslims in Their Places of Worship
Ruling prohibiting swearing non-Muslims in their temples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swearing non-Muslims in their places of worship, explain the prohibition, and identify the rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī) that supports it.

Core Idea
A judge may not enter a non-Muslim place of worship to administer an oath; the oath must be given elsewhere.

This passage addresses a specific procedural matter in judicial oaths: when a non-Muslim litigant must swear, should the judge go to the litigant's place of worship? The Hanafi ruling is clear and unambiguous: the judge is prohibited from entering a synagogue, church, or fire temple for this purpose. The rational proof is provided: entering such places is itself forbidden, so the judge cannot go there. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the judge administering an oath to a non-Muslim in that non-Muslim's place of worship. The ruling is that it is forbidden (haram).
Focus on the judge's action, not the non-Muslim's.
2
Locate the prohibition
The text states: 'قاضي کے لئے ان لوگوں کے عبادت خانے میں جانا ممنوع ہے' – it is prohibited (mamnu') for the judge to enter their places of worship.
The prohibition applies specifically to the judge, not to a general Muslim.
3
Understand the rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī)
The proof is simple: since the judge is forbidden from entering the place of worship, he cannot go there to administer the oath. The text frames it as a logical consequence: 'تو ان کے عبادت خانے میں جا کر کیسے قسم دیں گے؟' – how can he go and swear them?
This is a rational inference from the established prohibition.
4
Apply the ruling
The oath must be administered in the court or another Islamic venue. The judge should not travel to a non-Muslim's place of worship for this purpose.
This applies to all non-Muslims whether Jews, Christians, or Magians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يستحلفون في بيوت عبادتهم 27731 (44) اور قسم نہ دی جائے ان کو ان کے عبادت خانوں میں۔ یہودی کو یہودی کے عبادت خانے میں جاکر اور عیسائی کو عیسائی کے عبادت خانے میں جا کر یا مجوسی کو مجوسی کے عبادت خانے میں جا کر قسم نہ دیں۔ قاضی کے لئے ان لوگوں کے عبادت خانے میں جانا ممنوع ہے اس لئے ان کے عبادت خانے میں جا کر کیسے قسم دیں گے۔
And they shall not be made to swear in their places of worship. (44) And do not administer the oath to them in their places of worship. Do not go to the Jew's synagogue, the Christian's church, or the Magian's fire temple to administer the oath. It is forbidden for the judge to enter these places of worship; therefore, how can he go there and administer the oath?
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This ruling states that when swearing a non-Muslim litigant, the judge should not go to the non-Muslim's place of worship (synagogue, church, or fire temple) to administer the oath. The reason is that it is prohibited (mamnu') for the judge to enter such places. This prohibition likely stems from the sanctity of Islamic rulings and the judge's role. The oath should be administered in the court or another appropriate location, not in a place of worship of another religion.

Key Terms
بيوت عبادتهم (buyūt 'ibādatihim): Places of worship of non-Muslims: synagogues, churches, fire temples.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why should a judge not administer an oath to a non-Muslim in his place of worship?
A) Because the non-Muslim would not respect the oath there.
B) Because it is forbidden for the judge to enter such places.
C) Because the judge must remain in the courthouse.
D) Because it would give undue honor to the non-Muslim's religion.

Key Takeaway
It is haram for a judge to enter a non-Muslim place of worship to administer an oath; the oath must be given elsewhere.

The Ruling on Solemnizing Oaths by Time or Place: Not Obligatory but Recommended
Ruling on oath solemnization with dalils

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on solemnizing a Muslim's oath by time or place, and explain why the evidence does not make it obligatory.

Core Idea
Solemnizing an oath by the minbar or after Asr is recommended, not required; the oath is valid without it.

When a Muslim litigant is to take an oath, should the judge take him to the Prophet's minbar or wait until after Asr to add solemnity? This lesson examines the Hanafi position that such solemnization (taghlīẓ) is not obligatory but is a commendable practice. We will study the two key hadiths cited in the source and understand how they support the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the opening sentence: 'ولا يجب تغليظ اليمين على المسلم بزمان ولا بمكان'. State the ruling in your own words: it is not obligatory to make the oath solemn by time or place.
The ruling applies to a Muslim; non-Muslims may be treated differently.
2
Locate the Prools
The author provides two hadiths: one about swearing at the Prophet's minbar (from Jabir), and one about swearing after Asr (from Abu Huraira). Identify these in the margin note.
The minbar hadith is from Ibn Majah and Abu Dawud; the Asr hadith is also from Abu Dawud and Ibn Majah.
3
Analyze the Wajh al-Dalalah
Explain how each hadith indicates solemnization but does not command it. The minbar hadith warns of severe punishment for a false oath at the minbar, showing its solemnity. The Asr hadith lists swearing falsely after Asr among those Allah will not speak to, again showing solemnity. Neither hadith uses imperative language (amr) to make it obligatory.
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-wujub, but these hadiths do not contain direct commands.
4
Distinguish Wajib from Sunnah
The text explicitly says 'ولا يجب' (it is not obligatory) and then says 'البتہ ایسا کرے تو بہتر ہے' (but doing so is better). This clearly places the act in the category of sunnah (recommended), not wajib.
In Hanafi fiqh, sunnah muakkadah is a highly recommended act, omission of which is not sinful but blameworthy.
5
Apply the Ruling to a Scenario
Imagine a judge is about to administer an oath to a Muslim claimant. According to this ruling, the judge may choose to solemnize the oath by taking him to a mosque or after Asr, but it is not required. The oath is valid without any special time or place.
The ruling is specifically for Muslims; for non-Muslims, the judge may use solemnization if needed.

(45) And it is not obligatory to make the oath solemn for a Muslim by time or place. (45) It is not necessary to make the oath strong by time and place. Margin note: (A) The Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) said to Ibn Suriya: 'I remind you by Allah who saved you from the Pharaoh, split the sea, gave you shade, sent manna and quails, and sent the Torah to Moses. Do you find the ruling of stoning in your book?' Ibn Suriya said: 'You have reminded me of something so great that I cannot lie.' Taking the person to a specific place or a specific time, e.g., after Asr, to solemnize the oath is not obligatory. However, if one does so, it is better. The point: since the oath is by Allah, the restriction of mosque or Asr time is not necessary. But doing so is better. That is because there is a hadith about solemnization by the minbar: Jabir ibn Abdullah said: The Messenger of Allah (صلى الله عليه وسلم) said: 'Whoever swears a sinful oath by this minbar of mine, let him take his seat in the Fire, even if it is for a green twig.' (Ibn Majah, chapter on oath at the places of rights, p. 333, no. 2325; Abu Dawud, chapter on solemnizing oath at the Prophet's minbar, vol. 2, p. 07, no. 3226). This hadith indicates that solemnization is done by place (the minbar). And the proof for solemnization by time is the hadith: Abu Huraira said: The Messenger of Allah (صلى الله عليه وسلم) said: 'Three people Allah will not speak to on the Day of Judgment: a man who withholds surplus water from a wayfarer, and a man who swears falsely on a commodity after Asr.' (Abu Dawud, chapter on withholding water, p. 135, no. 4 347; Ibn Majah, chapter on dislike of swearing in sale and purchase, p. 316, no. 2207). From this hadith, it is understood that time (Asr) also solemnizes the oath.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage clarifies that for a Muslim litigant, the judge is not obliged to make the oath solemn by taking him to a specific place (like the Prophet's minbar) or a specific time (like after Asr). However, doing so is recommended (bihter/better). The commentary provides proofs: the hadith about the minbar shows that swearing at the Prophet's minbar is more solemn and carries greater consequence if false. The hadith about after Asr shows that swearing falsely after Asr leads to divine punishment. Despite these, the requirement is not compulsory; the oath itself by Allah is sufficient. The margin note (الف) recounts the story of Ibn Suriya to illustrate how the Prophet solemnized an oath by reminding the Jew of past favors, but this is for non-Muslims. For Muslims, the ruling is as stated.

Key Terms
تغليظ اليمين (taghlīẓ al-yamīn): Solemnizing the oath by additional measures like time or place.
منبر (minbar): The pulpit of the Prophet in his mosque.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is it obligatory to solemnize a Muslim's oath by taking him to the Prophet's minbar or after Asr?
A) Yes, it is wajib.
B) No, it is not wajib but recommended.
C) It is haram to do so.
D) Only for non-Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Solemnizing a Muslim's oath by specific times or places is a commendable practice, not a legal requirement; the oath itself suffices.

Evidence in Sale Disputes: When One Party Witnesses
Ruling with evidence on sale disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the party who presents evidence in a dispute over sale price or quantity wins, and identify which party is considered the claimant.

Core Idea
Whichever party brings evidence wins—each party may be a claimant in part.

In a sale, disagreements over price or quantity can arise. The Hanafi school resolves this by examining who brings witnesses. This lesson explains that both buyer and seller can be considered claimants in their respective claims, and the one who provides evidence (bayyinah) will have his claim accepted. The ruling is grounded in the prophetic hadith: 'Evidence is on the claimant and the oath is on the defendant.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Determine whether the dispute is over price (buyer claims lower, seller claims higher) or quantity (seller admits some, buyer claims more).
2
Locate each party's claim
Map each party's assertion: for price, see who is claiming an increase; for quantity, see who claims more than admitted. That party becomes the claimant (mudda'i) for that part.
3
Apply the rule of bayyinah
Whichever party brings evidence (two male witnesses or one man and two women) on their claim, their evidence is accepted. The other party becomes the defendant and must take an oath if the claimant has no evidence.
4
Cite the hadith proof
The hadith 'al-bayyinatu 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yaminu 'ala al-mudda'a 'alayhi' assigns the burden of proof to the claimant. Since both can be claimants in overlapping claims, each must prove their part.
Hadith from Tirmidhi no. 1341.
5
Conclude the ruling
The judge decides in favor of the party who presents evidence. The other party's claim is dismissed unless they also produce evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۵۵) واذا اختلف المتبايعان في البيع فادعى المشترى ثمنا وادعى البائع اكثر منه او اعترف البائع بقدر من المبيع وادعى المشترى اكثر منه واقام احدهما البينة قضى له بها
بائع اور مشتری نے اختلاف کیا بیع میں ۔ پس مشتری نے دعوی کیا ایک شمن کا اور بائع نے دعوی کیا اس سے زیادہ کا۔ یا اعتراف کیا بائع نے بیع کی ایک مقدار کا اور مشتری دعوی کرے اس سے زاید کا۔ اور دونوں میں سے ایک نے بینہ قائم کیا تو اسی کے لئے اس کا فیصلہ ہوگا۔
ا یہ دونوں مسئلے اس اصول پر ہیں کہ یہاں بائع اور مشتری دونوں مدعی بھی بن سکتے ہیں اور مدعی علیہ بھی بن سکتے ہیں۔ چونکہ دونوں مدعی ہیں اس لئے جو بھی بینہ قائم کرے گا اس کے بینہ کے مطابق فیصلہ کر دیا جائے گا اور دوسرے کو مدعی علیہ قرار دیا جائے گا۔ مثلا مشتری دعوی کرتا ہے کہ دس درہم میں یہ بکری خریدی ہے اس لئے یہ بکری دے دو تو مشتری مدعی ہوا اور بائع مدعی علیہ ہو گیا۔ اس صورت میں مشتری کا بینہ قبول کرنا چاہئے اور بائع پر قسم ہوگی۔ لیکن بائع اس سے زیادہ کا دعوی کرتا ہے مثلا بارہ درہم کا دعوی کرتا ہے کہ مجھے بارہ درہم دیں اور بکری لے جائیں۔ اس اعتبار سے بائع مدعی ہوا اور مشتری مدعی علیہ ہو گیا۔ اس لئے بائع کا بینہ قبول کرنا چاہئے۔ اس لئے جو بھی بینہ پیش کرے گا اس کو مدعی مان کر اس کی گواہی قبول کر کے اس کے حق میں فیصلہ کر دیا جائے گا۔
دوسرے مسئلے میں بائع دعوی کرتا ہے ایک متعین مقدار بیع کا مثلا بائع کہتا ہے کہ دس درہم میں دس کیلو گیہوں بیچا ہوں۔ اس لئے دس درہم دو اور دس کیلو گیہوں لے جاؤ۔ اس اعتبار سے بائع مدعی ہوا اور مشتری مدعی علیہ ہوا اس لئے بائع کے بینہ کا اعتبار ہونا چاہئے ۔ اور مشتری اس سے زیادہ مبیع کا دعوی کرتا ہے مثلا کہتا ہے کہ دس درہم میں بارہ کیلو گیہوں کی بات ہوئی تھی۔ اس اعتبار سے مشتری مدعی ہوا اور بائع مدعی علیہ ہوا۔ اس لئے مشتری کے بینہ کو قبول کرنا چاہئے۔
ان دونوں مسائل میں دونوں مدعی اور دونوں مدعی علیہ بن سکتے ہیں اس لئے جو بھی بینہ قائم کرے گا اس کے بینہ کو قبول کر کے اس کے حق میں فیصلہ کر دیا جائے گا۔ حدیث گزر چکی ہے۔ عن عمر بن شعيب عن ابيه عن جده ان النبي الله قال في خطبته البينة على المدعى واليمين على المدعى عليه (الف) ( ترمذی شریف، باب ما جاء فی ان البینۃ علی المدعی والیمین علی المدعی علیہ ص ۲۴۹ نمبر ۱۳۴۱) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ مدعی پر گواہ اور مدعی علیہ پر قسم ہے۔
حاشیہ : (الف) آپ نے خطبے میں فرمایا گواہ دعوی پر ہے اور قسم مدعی علیہ پر ہے۔
And if the buyer and seller disagree in the sale, and the buyer claims a price and the seller claims more than that, or the seller acknowledges a quantity of the merchandise and the buyer claims more than that, and one of them produces evidence, judgment is given for him. (Urdu commentary expands on the rule: both parties can be claimants and defendants; the one who produces evidence is treated as claimant and his evidence is accepted; the other is the defendant. The hadith is quoted: 'The evidence is on the claimant and the oath is on the defendant.')
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This is a ruling on sale disputes when one party has evidence. The text states that if the buyer and seller disagree on price or quantity, and one of them brings witnesses, the judge decides in his favor. The commentary clarifies that in such disputes, both parties can be considered claimants in their respective claims. For example, if the buyer says the price was 10 dirhams and the seller says 12, the buyer is the claimant for the goods at 10 dirhams, making the seller the defendant; but the seller is the claimant for the extra 2 dirhams. So whichever party brings evidence—whether for his claim or against the other's—his evidence is accepted. The hadith from Tirmidhi supports this: the prophet ﷺ said in a sermon, 'Evidence is on the claimant, and the oath is on the defendant.'

Key Terms
البيع (al-bay'): The contract of sale.
المشتري (al-mushtari): The buyer in the sale contract.
البائع (al-ba'i'): The seller in the sale contract.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence, typically two witnesses.
المدعي (al-mudda'i): The claimant who must produce evidence.
المدعى عليه (al-mudda'a 'alayhi): The defendant who takes an oath if the claimant has no evidence.
اليمين (al-yamin): The oath taken by the defendant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In sale disputes over price or quantity, whichever party brings evidence wins, because each party can be a claimant for the extra they demand.

Preference for Evidence of Increase
Ruling when both buyer and seller present evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when both parties present evidence, the evidence proving an increase is preferred, and explain the underlying rationale.

Core Idea
The party claiming an increase is the true claimant; their evidence wins.

When both buyer and seller produce witnesses, the judge must decide whose evidence to accept. The Hanafi school provides a clear criterion: favor the evidence that proves an increase in price or quantity. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this rule and shows how it resolves conflicting claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting claims
Recognize that both parties are simultaneously claimants and defendants. Each asserts a different price or quantity.
For example, buyer says 10 dirhams, seller says 12 dirhams.
2
Determine who claims an increase
Compare the two claims. The party asserting a higher price or larger quantity is claiming an increase (ziyadah) over what the other admits.
Here the seller claims an extra 2 dirhams.
3
Apply the preference rule
The evidence of the party who proves the increase is preferred (awla). The judge accepts their witnesses and rules accordingly.
The seller's evidence wins because it proves an increase.
4
Understand the rationale
The party claiming the increase is the real claimant (mudda'i) for that excess; the other party is the defendant (mudda'a 'alayh). The burden of proof is on the claimant, and their evidence prevails.
This avoids stalemate and follows the principle that certainty outweighs doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان اقام كل واحد منهما البينة كانت البينة المثبتة للزيادة اولى
چونکہ ان مسئلوں میں دونوں مدعی اور دونوں مدعی علیہ ہیں اس لئے دونوں بینہ قائم کر سکتے ہیں۔ اب دونوں نے بینہ قائم کر دیا تو کس کے بینہ کو ترجیح دیں گے۔ تو فرماتے ہیں کہ جس کا بینہ زیادتی کو ثابت کرتا ہو وہ اصل میں زیادتی کا مدعی ہے اور دوسرا اس کا انکار کرتا ہے اس
If each of them produces evidence, the evidence that proves an increase is more deserving. (Urdu commentary: Since both are claimants and defendants, both can present evidence. Now both have presented evidence, so whose evidence do we prefer? They say: the one whose evidence proves an increase is actually the claimant for the increase, and the other denies it.)
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This ruling addresses the scenario where both parties have witnesses. The judge must prefer the evidence that substantiates an increase—either in price or in quantity. The commentary explains that the party proving the increase is the real claimant for that excess, while the other party is denying it. For example, if the buyer claims a lower price and the seller claims a higher price, the seller's evidence for the higher price is preferred because he is proving an increase.

Key Terms
الزيادة (al-ziyadah): The increase in price or quantity claimed by one party.
اولى (awla): More deserving, preferred in acceptance of evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule resolves conflicting evidence by preferring the proof of increase, as the party claiming the extra amount is the true claimant.

Judge Procedure When Neither Party Has Evidence
Ruling when neither party has evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedure when neither buyer nor seller has evidence in a sale dispute and explain the judge's reasoning.

Core Idea
If neither party has evidence, the judge offers each a choice: accept the other's claim or face rescission.

When a sale dispute reaches the court and both parties lack witnesses, the Hanafi judge does not immediately cancel the contract. Instead, he gives each party a final opportunity to concede to the other's claim under the threat of rescission. This ruling reflects the principle of preserving contracts where possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the ruling applies when both the buyer and seller are unable to produce witnesses (bayyinah) for their claims regarding the price or the object of sale.
The absence of evidence from both sides triggers the procedure.
2
Understand the judge's first offer
The judge addresses the buyer: either accept the seller's claimed price, or the sale will be rescinded (faskh). This is an attempt to induce agreement.
The threat of cancellation may pressure the buyer to concede.
3
Understand the judge's second offer
Similarly, the judge addresses the seller: either hand over the quantity or item claimed by the buyer, or the sale will be rescinded.
The judge tries both sides to preserve the contract.
4
Recognize the rationale
The judge hopes that the fear of rescission will motivate one party to accept the other's claim, thereby preserving the sale. This aligns with the Hanafi principle of preferring contract continuity.
If both refuse, the sale is cancelled.
5
Note the outcome if both refuse
If neither party accepts, the judge rescinds the sale. The contract is voided, and each party returns whatever they received.
Rescission is a last resort.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان لم يكن لكل واحد منهما بينة قيل للمشترى اما ان ترضى بالثمن الذى ادعاه البائع والا فسخنا البيع وقيل للبائع اما ان تسلّم ما ادعاه المشترى من المبيع والا فسخنا البيع
پس اگر دونوں میں سے کسی کے لئے بینہ نہ ہو تو مشتری سے کہا جائے گا کہ یا راضی ہو جائے اس قیمت پر جس کا بائع دعوی کرتا ہے ورنہ تو ہم بیع فسخ کر دیں گے۔ اور بائع سے کہا جائے گا یا مان لے اس بات کو جس کا مشتری دعوی کرتا ہے مبیع میں سے ورنہ تو ہم بیع فسخ کر دیں گے۔
اگر دونوں میں سے کسی کے پاس بینہ نہیں ہے تو حاکم پہلے مسئلے میں مشتری سے کہے گا کہ یا تو بائع جتنا کہتا ہے مثلا بارہ درہم کا دعوی ہے تو بارہ درہم کو مان لو اور بکری لے لو ورنہ بیع فسخ کر دیں گے۔ یہ اس لئے کہے گا تا کہ ہوسکتا ہے کہ ڈر سے مشتری مان لے اور بیع باقی رکھے۔ اس طرح دوسرے مسئلے میں بائع سے کہے گا کہ یا تو مشتری جتنے مبیع کا دعوی کرتا ہے مثلا دس درہم میں بارہ کیلو گیہوں کا تو اس کو مان لو اور بیع باقی رکھو ورنہ تو بیع فسخ کر دوں گا۔ یہ اس لئے کہ ممکن ہے کہ بیع ٹوٹنے کے خوف سے بائع مشتری کی بات مان لے۔
تسلم: بات مان لے تسلیم کرلے۔
If neither of them has evidence, it is said to the buyer: either be satisfied with the price that the seller claims, or we will rescind the sale. And it is said to the seller: either hand over what the buyer claims of the merchandise, or we will rescind the sale. (Urdu commentary: The judge asks the buyer to accept the seller's claim or the sale is rescinded; similarly, the seller is asked to accept the buyer's claim or the sale is rescinded. This is done in the hope that one party will agree to preserve the sale. 'tassallum' means acceptance.)
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This ruling deals with the situation where neither party can produce witnesses. The judge first attempts to resolve the dispute by asking one party to accept the other's claim. For example, if the seller claims a higher price, the buyer is told to either pay that price or the sale will be cancelled. Similarly, if the buyer claims more quantity, the seller is told to deliver that quantity or the sale will be cancelled. The commentary explains this is a strategic move: the threat of cancellation may induce one party to concede, thus preserving the contract. If they still do not agree, the next step is oaths.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or cancellation of the contract.
تسلم (tasallum): To accept or receive what is claimed.

No Agreement: Both Parties Take Oaths and Sale Is Cancelled
Ruling when parties do not agree and have no evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a sale dispute has no evidence and both parties swear, and identify the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
When both parties swear, the sale is cancelled — no claim stands against an oath.

This lesson deals with a sale dispute where neither party has witnesses and they refuse to agree. The Hanafi procedure provides a decisive outcome: the judge administers oaths to both, starting with the buyer, and if both swear, the sale is rescinded. This ruling is based on a hadith and ensures a clear resolution when evidence is absent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Recognize the conditions: a sale contract exists, a dispute over the subject matter, no evidence (bayyinah) from either party, and they refuse to agree.
2
Understand the oath procedure
The judge makes each party take an oath on the other's claim, beginning with the buyer. The order (buyer first) is prescribed.
The buyer swears first because he is the one claiming ownership against the seller's claim.
3
Apply the ruling after both swear
After both have sworn, the judge must cancel (faskh) the sale. No other verdict is possible because the oaths create equal claims with no way to prefer one.
Cancellation is the only logical outcome — the property returns to the seller and the buyer gets his money back.
4
Connect the hadith evidence
The Prophet ﷺ instructed two disputants with no evidence to 'cast lots for the oath', indicating that both must swear. The Hanafi school deduces that when both swear, no claim stands, so the contract is voided.
The dalil is a hadith from Abu Huraira (transmitted by Abu Dawood).

If they do not agree, the judge makes each of them take an oath on the other's claim, and begins with the buyer's oath. If they both swear, the judge cancels their sale. (Urdu commentary: Since both are defendants and the claimant has no evidence, both are made to swear on the other's claim. Since both have sworn and there is no way to prefer one, the sale is rescinded. A hadith supports this: Abu Huraira reported that two men disputed over merchandise with no evidence, and the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Cast lots for the oath, whether they like it or not.')
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This final ruling addresses the case where both parties refuse to accept the other's claim and have no evidence. The judge administers oaths to both: first the buyer swears regarding the seller's claim, then the seller swears regarding the buyer's claim. Since both have taken oaths, no decision can be made in favor of one, so the sale is cancelled. The commentary cites a hadith where the Prophet ﷺ instructed two disputants with no evidence to cast lots for the oath, indicating that both must swear. This procedure is unique to Hanafi fiqh and emphasizes the finality when evidence is absent and agreement fails.

Key Terms
استخلف (istahlafa): To make someone take an oath formally in court.
اليمين (al-yamin): The oath, here used for both parties.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of the sale contract.
استهما (istahama): To cast lots or draw straws for determining who takes the oath first.

Key Takeaway
When both parties in a sale dispute take oaths without evidence, the sale is cancelled.

The Six Types of Testimony in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining testimony and classifying it into six categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six types of testimony (shahada) according to the Hanafi school and identify the required number and gender of witnesses for each type, based on Quranic proof.

Core Idea
Testimony is classified into six categories, each with specific witness requirements derived from the Quran.

Testimony (shahada) is a fundamental legal proof in Islamic law. This passage establishes its legitimacy from three Quranic verses and then presents a six-fold classification based on the subject matter and the conditions of the witnesses. Understanding this classification is essential for applying the correct rules in court or legal proceedings according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic Proofs
Locate the three Quranic verses cited at the beginning of the passage. Observe that each verse relates to a different context: financial transactions (Baqarah 2:282), false accusation (Nur 24:13), and general witnessing (Talaq 65:2).
These verses provide the foundational legitimacy for the institution of testimony.
2
List the Six Types
Read the six types sequentially. For each type, note the specific witness requirements: number, gender, and required level of justice (adalah).
The types are distinguished by the nature of the case: zina, other hudud/qisas, transactions, shatr al-shahada, khabar, and women's exclusive matters.
3
Understand Shatr al-Shahada and Khabar
The passage explains that shatr al-shahada (half testimony) is considered news (khabar), not full testimony. It applies when either the condition of justice or masculinity is partially met. Note the specific examples given: informing about an agency or dismissal.
This category shows the Hanafi nuance in balancing evidentiary requirements with practical necessity.
4
Recognize the Rationale
Reflect on why each type has its specific requirements. For instance, zina requires four men because of its severity, while women's testimony is accepted in transactions to facilitate commerce, and exclusively in matters like childbirth where men are usually unaware.
The Hanafi classification is driven by the subject matter and the likelihood of obtaining reliable evidence.

Book of Testimonies. Book of Testimonies. Essential notes: Shahadat is the plural of shahada, meaning giving testimony. Its proof is in these verses: 'And call upon two witnesses from among your men. And if there are not two men, then a man and two women from among those you approve as witnesses, so that if one of them errs, the other may remind her.' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:282) Another verse: 'Why did they not bring four witnesses for it? So when they do not bring the witnesses, then they are the liars in the sight of Allah.' (Surah al-Nur 24:13) And a third verse: 'And take witnesses of justness from among you, and establish the testimony for Allah. That is what you are exhorted with.' (Surah al-Talaq 65:2) By these verses, testimony is established. Testimony has six types: (1) The first type is testimony for zina. This is the highest. For it, the testimony of four men is a condition. The testimony of women will not suffice for proving it. And all must be just. (2) The second type is testimony for the remaining hudud and qisas. For proving them, two just men are required. In this, the testimony of women is also not acceptable. (3) The third type is testimony for transactions. To prove them, two just men or one just man and two just women are required. For their proof, the testimony of women is also sufficient. However, the testimony of purely women is not accepted. (4) The fourth type is shatr al-shahada (half of testimony). That is, one just man or two men of obscure status (mustur al-hal) are acceptable. In reality, giving testimony has two parts: one being just and the other being male. This is called shatr (half). Therefore, either one just man or two of obscure status suffice. This situation is not in reality testimony but rather news. For this reason, it is not sufficient to prove transactions and contracts. For example, giving news that so-and-so has appointed you as his agent for a matter, or has dismissed an agent. In this, the news of one just man or two of obscure status suffices. (5) The fifth type is news (khabar). In this, the news of a child or a slave woman is also sufficient. For example, a child brings food to a teacher and gives news that 'my mother has sent this as a gift for you', then for the teacher, eating this food is permissible. Or a slave woman gives news that 'my master has sent a gift for you', then for the person it was sent to, eating that food is lawful. So in these small matters, the news of a slave woman and a child is acceptable. (6) The sixth type is where men cannot be informed, such as childbirth etc. There, only the testimony of women is accepted due to necessity. Footnote: (a) 'Take testimony of two men from among you...' (b) 'Why did they not bring four witnesses...' (c) 'And take witnesses of justness from among you...'
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage introduces Kitab al-Shahadat (Book of Testimony) by first establishing its legitimacy from three Quranic verses: Surah al-Baqarah 2:282 (for financial transactions), Surah al-Nur 24:13 (for the hadd of qadhf), and Surah al-Talaq 65:2 (generally for witnessing). The author then presents a classification of testimony into six types, each with specific requirements. The classification is based on the subject matter: (1) adultery (zina) requires four just men, no women; (2) other hadd penalties and qisas require two just men; (3) financial transactions (mu'amalat) require two just men or one just man and two just women; (4) 'shatr al-shahada' (half testimony) is a category where the witness lacks either full justice or full masculinity—either one just man or two men of obscure status (mustur al-hal) suffice, but this is considered 'news' (khabar) rather than formal testimony, so it only confirms matters like agency and dismissal; (5) 'khabar' (news) from children or slave women is accepted in trivial matters like gifts of food; (6) matters where men are usually unaware, like childbirth, accept only women's testimony. The footnote provides Urdu translations of the Quranic verses. This classification reflects the Hanafi school's nuanced approach to testimony, balancing the need for proof with practical necessity.

Key Terms
شہادت (shahada): Testimony; giving evidence in a legal context. Plural: shahadat.
مستور الحال (mustur al-hal): A person whose inner uprightness is unknown but whose outward appearance is acceptable; of obscure status.
شطر الشھادۃ (shatr al-shahada): Half of testimony; a reduced form where either the condition of justness or masculinity is partially absent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies testimony into six types based on the subject matter, with varying witness requirements for gender and number, all grounded in Quranic evidence.

Obligation of Testimony When Called Upon
States the ruling on obligatory testimony with Quran and Hadith proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which testimony becomes obligatory on a witness and identify the exception for hudud and qisas.

Core Idea
Testimony is obligatory when the claimant has no other proof and demands it—concealment is sinful.

This lesson establishes a key ruling in the law of testimony. While testifying is generally a virtuous act, it becomes a binding duty when a claimant's right depends solely on your knowledge. The source passage presents the ruling, the Quranic and prophetic proofs, and a significant exception for hudud and qisas cases. Understanding this distinction ensures you know when a witness must speak and when they may remain silent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The passage states that testimony is fard (obligatory) on witnesses. Note the condition: the claimant demands it and there is no other witness.
The obligation is not absolute; it depends on the situation.
2
Locate the dalil – Quranic verses
Two verses from Surah al-Baqarah (2:282–283) are cited: 'Let not the witnesses refuse when they are called' and 'Do not conceal testimony'. The imperative form indicates obligation in Hanafi usul.
The emphasis is on the command (amr) which yields wujub.
3
Understand the hadith proof
The hadith praises the one who brings his testimony before being asked, showing the virtue of volunteering, but the obligation arises when called.
This supplementary proof reinforces the duty.
4
Note the exception for hudud and qisas
The passage explicitly excludes hudud (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation) from the obligation. In those cases, the witness has a choice.
This exception preserves the principle of avoiding doubtful punishments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١) الشهادة فرض تلزم الشهود ولا يسعهم كتمانها اذا طالبهم المدعى [۲۸۱۱](١) گواہی دینا فرض ہے۔ گواہوں کو لازم ہے اور اس کو چھپانے کی گنجائش نہیں ہے اگر ان سے مدعی اس کا مطالبہ کرے۔ ا ان گواہوں کے علاوہ کوئی اور گواہ نہیں ہے اور مدعی گواہوں سے گواہی دینے کا مطالبہ کر رہا ہے تو ان گواہوں پر گواہی دینا فرض ہے۔ عام معاملات میں گواہی چھپانے کی گنجائش نہیں ہے۔ چونکہ اور گواہ نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے اگر اس نے گواہی نہیں دی تو مدعی کا حق ضائع ہو جائے گا۔ اس لئے اس کو حق دلوانے کے لئے گواہی دینا فرض ہے (۲) آیت میں اس کی ترغیب ہے۔ ولا یاب الشهداء اذا ما دعوا ( آیت ۲۸۲ ، سورة البقرة ٢) ولا تكتموا الشهادة ومن يكتمها فانه آثم قلبه (الف ) ( آیت ۲۸۳) سورة البقرة ٢) ان دونوں آیتوں میں ہے کہ مدعی بلائے تو گواہ گواہی دینے سے انکار نہ کرے۔ اور یہ بھی ہے کہ گواہی چھپائے نہیں۔ اگر چھپایا تو گنہگار ہوگا۔ حدیث میں ترغیب ہے۔ عن زيد بن خالد الجهني ان النبي لله قال الا اخبركم بخير الشهداء؟ الذي ياتي بشهادته قبل ان يسألها (ب) (مسلم شریف، باب بیان خیر الشهود، ج ۲، صے سے نمبر ۷۱۹ اسرا بو داؤد شریف، باب فی الشهادة ، ج ۲ س ۱۵۰، نمبر ۳۵۹۶) و یہ صورت حال معاملات میں ہے۔ البتہ حدود اور قصاص میں گواہی دینے اور گواہی چھپانے کا اختیار ہے ۔
(1) Testimony is an obligation binding upon witnesses, and they are not permitted to conceal it when the claimant demands it from them. [2811] (1) Giving testimony is obligatory. It is binding upon witnesses, and there is no scope to conceal it if the claimant demands it from them. If there are no other witnesses besides these, and the claimant is demanding testimony from them, then giving testimony is obligatory upon these witnesses. In general transactions, concealing testimony is not permissible. Since there are no other witnesses, if he does not give testimony, the claimant's right will be lost. Therefore, for the purpose of securing his right, giving testimony is obligatory. (2) In the verse, there is encouragement for this: 'And let not the witnesses refuse when they are called' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:282) and 'Do not conceal testimony, and whoever conceals it, his heart is sinful' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:283). Both verses indicate that when the claimant calls, the witness should not refuse to give testimony, and also that testimony should not be concealed; if concealed, he will be sinful. There is encouragement in the hadith: From Zayd ibn Khalid al-Juhani that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Shall I inform you of the best of witnesses? The one who brings his testimony before being asked for it.' (Muslim, Book of Testimonies, Vol. 2, p. [؟] no. 719; Abu Dawud, Book of Testimony, Vol. 2, p. 150, no. 3596) This situation applies to general transactions. However, in hudud and qisas, there is a choice between giving or concealing testimony.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on the obligation of testimony in general transactions. The ruling is that when witnesses are the only ones who know the truth and the claimant demands their testimony, it becomes obligatory (fard) upon them to testify. The evidence is from the Quranic verses that command witnesses not to refuse when called and forbid concealment of testimony, and from the hadith praising those who volunteer testimony. The Urdu commentary explains that this obligation applies only when there is no other witness; otherwise, the claimant's right would be lost. However, the passage notes an exception: in cases of hudud (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation), the witness has a choice, which will be explained in the next lesson.

Key Terms
الشهادة (al-shahada): Testimony given in court to establish a right or claim.
فرض (fard): An obligation that is binding; omission is sinful.
حدود (hudud): Fixed punishments prescribed by Islamic law for certain crimes (e.g., theft, adultery).
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation or retaliation in kind for intentional homicide or bodily harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does testimony become obligatory on a witness?
A) At any time, even without a demand
B) When the witness is the only one who knows the truth and the claimant demands it
C) Only when the judge issues a subpoena
D) Only in hudud and qisas cases

Key Takeaway
Testimony is a binding duty when the claimant's right depends solely on your knowledge and they demand it, except in hudud and qisas where the witness has a choice.

Testimony in Hudud: The Choice to Conceal or Disclose
States that witnesses have a choice between concealing and disclosing in hudud cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a witness must testify in hudud cases and explain why concealment is recommended.

Core Idea
In hudud, a witness is not required to testify; concealment is better to preserve life and limb.

Unlike financial transactions where testimony is often obligatory, hudud cases carry severe punishments that can cost a person their life or limb. This lesson examines the unique Hanafi position that a witness in hudud has a choice between concealing the sin or testifying, with concealment being the recommended option. We will explore the evidence from hadith that supports this ruling and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is testimony in hudud cases. The ruling is that the witness has a choice (mubah) between concealment and disclosure, with concealment being recommended (mustahabb).
This is a unique ruling specific to hudud.
2
Locate the dalil
The evidence comes from the hadith of Ma'iz and Hazzal in Sunan Abi Dawud. The Prophet (ﷺ) expressed regret that Ma'iz was not left to escape, and told Hazzal that covering him would have been better.
These ahadith are ahad, but accepted as proof.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's statement 'Why did you not leave him?' indicates disapproval of forcing the hudud enforcement and implies that concealment is better.
The illa (reason) is avoiding harm to the person.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools may hold that testimony in hudud is obligatory when known, but the Hanafi school allows concealment to avoid the severe penalties.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The mufta bihi position is that concealment is preferred. This is based on the principle of dar' al-hudud (warding off hudud) and the hadith evidence.
The authority weight is zahir al-riwayah.

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on testimony in hudud (fixed punishments such as zina, theft, etc.). Unlike general transactions where testimony is obligatory, in hudud the witness has a choice (mukhayyar) between concealing the sin or testifying. The preferred option (afdal) is concealment (al-sitr) to protect the accused from the severe penalties of hudud. Another hadith encourages covering the faults of others. The commentary also mentions a hadith about a thief where the prophet sought to avoid the amputation by suggesting the confession was mistaken. This shows the principle of avoiding hudud when possible.

Testimony in Theft: Word It Carefully
Ruling on testimony wording in theft

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligatory wording for testimony in theft and explain why the Hanafi school requires this phrasing.

Core Idea
In theft testimony, say 'he took property' — not 'he stole' — to protect both the owner's right and the accused's hand.

This lesson addresses a subtle but important point in Islamic testimony: the wording a witness should use in a theft case. The Hanafi school prescribes a specific phrasing that ensures the owner can reclaim his property while avoiding the harsh hadd punishment. Understanding this ruling requires appreciating the balance between the rights of Allah and the rights of people.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is testimony in a theft case. The ruling is wajib (obligatory) to phrase the testimony in a specific way.
2
State the required wording
The witness must say 'he took the property' (akhadha al-mal) and not 'he stole' (saraqa).
3
Understand the rationale
Theft involves two rights: the right of Allah (hadd punishment) and the right of the owner (property return). The wording 'took' focuses on the owner's right, allowing property recovery without triggering the hadd.
This is based on istihsan to combine both rights.
4
Apply the ruling
In practice, a witness should carefully choose words to avoid automatic amputation while still testifying to the taking of property.

(3) But it is obligatory to testify concerning property in theft: he should say 'he took the property' and not say 'he stole'. [2813] (4) Testimony has several ranks. Among them is testimony in zina: it requires four men, and women's testimony is not accepted in it. It has passed earlier that testimony has six ranks. Among them, the highest rank is testimony in zina, which requires four just men; women's testimony is not acceptable in it. The proof for four witnesses is this verse: 'And those who commit immorality among your women, bring four witnesses against them from among you...' (Surah an-Nisa 4:15) Another verse says: 'Why did they not bring four witnesses? And if they do not bring witnesses, then they are the liars before Allah.' (Surah an-Nur 24:13) Both verses indicate that for the proof of zina, four witnesses are required. Women's testimony in hudud is not acceptable; the proof is the mursal hadith from az-Zuhri: 'The sunnah from the Messenger of Allah and the two caliphs after him is that women's testimony is not allowed in hudud.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, vol. 109, in the chapter on women's testimony in hudud)
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage covers two rulings. First, in cases of theft (sariqa), the witness should testify carefully: he should say 'He took the property' (akhadha al-mal) and not 'He stole' (saraqa). The reason is that theft entails two aspects: the hadd punishment (amputation of the hand) and the return of the property to the owner (rights of people). By testifying that the accused simply took the property, the witness ensures that the owner can recover his property, but avoids triggering the hadd punishment which would be enforced only if the witness explicitly states that theft occurred. This is a form of istihsan (juristic preference) to combine preserving both rights. Second, the passage begins to discuss the ranks of testimony, stating that the highest rank is testimony for zina which requires four male witnesses; women's testimony is not accepted in hudud. The evidence is from Quranic verses requiring four witnesses for zina and from the practice (sunnah) that women's testimony is not accepted in hudud. This second part is cut off but introduces the next topic.

Key Terms
السرقة (al-sariqa): Theft that triggers the hadd punishment when conditions are met.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school obliges witnesses in theft to phrase their testimony as 'he took the property' to avoid the hadd while preserving the owner's right to restitution.

Testimony in Zina: Four Male Witnesses Required
States the requirement and evidence for testimony in zina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the requirement for testimony in zina and identify the Quranic and hadith proofs that support it.

Core Idea
Zina requires four male witnesses; women's testimony is not accepted in hudud.

In this lesson, we examine the highest rank of testimony: that required for proving zina (adultery/fornication). The Hanafi school, based on clear Quranic verses and the established sunnah, stipulates four upright male witnesses. Women's testimony is entirely excluded in hudud cases. Understanding this strict evidentiary requirement is crucial for grasping the Islamic legal system's approach to protecting honor and preventing false accusations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is testimony in zina. The ruling is that it requires four male witnesses; women's testimony is not accepted.
This is the highest rank of testimony (مراتب الشهادة).
2
Locate the primary dalil
The primary proof is from the Quran: Surah an-Nisa 4:15 commands bringing four witnesses; Surah an-Nur 24:13 reinforces the requirement.
Both verses explicitly mention 'four witnesses'.
3
Interpret the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit command 'fa-stashhidu alayhinna arba'atan minkum' indicates that four is the minimum and that women are not included (since 'minkum' refers to men in context).
The imperative form shows obligation.
4
Examine the secondary dalil
A mursal hadith from az-Zuhri confirms that the sunnah from the Prophet and the two caliphs excludes women's testimony in hudud.
Mursal hadith is accepted in Hanafi usul for such matters.
5
Note the exclusion rule
Women's testimony is not accepted in any hudud case, not just zina. This maintains strict standards.
Hudud include zina, theft, etc., but the ruling is specific to each.

Commentary
This passage describes the highest rank of testimony: testimony in cases of zina (adultery/fornication). It requires four upright male witnesses; women's testimony is not accepted at all in hudud cases. Shahadat — Testimony

Testimony for Zina: Four Male Witnesses Required
Explains the rule and proofs for testimony in zina requiring four men

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for testimony in zina, cite the Quranic proofs for four witnesses, and explain why women's testimony is excluded based on the mursal hadith.

Core Idea
Zina requires four just men; women's testimony is not accepted in hudud.

This lesson examines the strictest category of testimony in Islamic law: testimony for zina. Unlike other crimes, zina requires four male witnesses, and women's testimony is entirely excluded. The proofs come directly from the Quran and from the established practice of the Prophet and the caliphs, transmitted via a mursal hadith. Understanding this rule protects the integrity of hudud punishments and prevents wrongful convictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Recognize that the passage presents testimony for zina as the highest rank of shahadat, requiring four male witnesses. Note that this applies specifically to hudud, not to other areas like financial transactions.
The rule is unique to zina among hudud.
2
Examine the Quranic proof
Read the two verses cited: Surah an-Nisa 4:15 and Surah an-Nur 24:13. Both explicitly command the production of four witnesses. In Hanafi usul, the imperative form (فاستشهدوا) indicates obligation, and the number four is a qat'i specification.
The dalalah (import) of these verses is qat'i (definitive).
3
Analyze the hadith proof for excluding women
The mursal hadith from al-Zuhri states that the practice (sunnah) of the Prophet and the two caliphs is that women's testimony is not accepted in hudud. Hanafis accept mursal hadith from trustworthy tabi'is as proof, especially when supported by practice.
Mursal hadith is a valid proof in the Hanafi school under these conditions.
4
Trace the legal effect (thamarah)
The practical consequence: if fewer than four male witnesses come forward, or if any are women, the testimony is rejected. This prevents qadhf (false accusation) and protects the accused.
If a woman testifies, her testimony is simply not counted toward the required four.
5
Confirm the cross-madhhab agreement
All major schools agree on the need for four male witnesses for zina. However, some schools (like Malikis) allow women's testimony in certain non-hudud cases; Hanafis restrict it to matters involving property and transactions.
The core rule is muttafaq alayh; details differ.

[۲۸۱۳] (۴) گواہی کے چند مرتبے ہیں۔ ان میں سے زنا کی گواہی ہے۔ اس میں اعتبار کیا جاتا ہے چار مرد اور نہیں قبول کی جاتی ہے اس میں عورت کی گواہی ۔ پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ گواہی کے چھ مرتبے ہیں۔ ان میں سے اعلی مرتبہ زنا کی گواہی ہے جن میں چار عادل مردوں کی گواہی قبول کی جاتی ہے۔ اس میں عورت کی گواہی قابل قبول نہیں ہے۔ چار گواہ کی دلیل یہ آیت ہے۔ والتي يأتين الفاحشة من نسائكم فاستشهدوا عليهن اربعة منكم فان شهدوا فامسكوهن في البيوت (ب) ( آیت ۱۵، سورۃ النساء ۴) دوسری آیت میں ہے۔ لولا جاء و عليه باربعة شهداء فاذ لم يأتوا بالشهداء فاولئك عند الله هم الكاذبون (ج ) ( آیت ۱۳ سورۃ النور ۲۴) ان دونوں آیتوں میں ہے کہ زنا کے ثبوت کے لئے چار گواہ چاہئے ۔ عورتوں میں حدود کی گواہی قابل قبول نہیں ہے اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث مرسل ہے۔ عن الزهري قال مضت السنة من رسول الله الله والـخـلـيـفـتـيـن من بعده الا تجوز شهادة النساء في الحدود (و) (مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ۱۰۹، في شهادة النساء في الحدود، ج... اس حدیث مرسل اور اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ حدود میں عورتوں کی گواہی مقبول نہیں ہے۔
Testimony has several ranks. Among them is testimony for zina. In it consideration is given to four men, and women's testimony is not accepted. It has passed before that there are six ranks of testimony. Among them the highest rank is testimony for zina, in which the testimony of four just men is accepted. Women's testimony is not acceptable in it. The proof for four witnesses is this verse: 'And those who commit the immoral act from among your women, bring against them four witnesses from among you, and if they testify, then confine them to houses' (Surah An-Nisa 4:15). Another verse says: 'Why did they not bring four witnesses? And since they did not bring witnesses, they are indeed liars in the sight of Allah' (Surah An-Nur 24:13). In both these verses, it is required that for establishing zina, four witnesses are needed. Women's testimony in hudud is not acceptable. The proof for that is this mursal hadith: 'From al-Zuhri who said: The sunnah has continued from the Messenger of Allah and the two caliphs after him that women's testimony is not permissible in hudud.' (Reference) From this mursal hadith and athar it is understood that women's testimony in hudud is not accepted.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof about testimony for zina. It is the highest rank of testimony, requiring four just (adil) men. Women's testimony is not accepted in zina. The proofs are two Quranic verses (4:15 and 24:13) which explicitly require four witnesses for zina. Additionally, a mursal hadith from al-Zuhri states that the practice since the Prophet and the two caliphs is that women's testimony is not permissible in hudud (including zina). The commentary emphasizes that this is a strict requirement unique to zina among hudud.

Key Terms
حديث مرسل (hadith mursal): A hadith where the Companion is omitted from the chain, attributed directly to the Prophet. Hanafis accept mursal hadith under certain conditions.
زنا (zina): Unlawful sexual intercourse requiring four witnesses for conviction.

Testimony in Hudud and Qisas: Two Men Required
Ruling with multiple proofs for witness requirements in serious crimes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for testimony in hudud (excluding zina) and qisas, and explain the proofs (athar and rational argument) that support it.

Core Idea
In hudud and qisas, only two male witnesses are accepted; women's testimony is excluded due to their forgetfulness and the gravity of these cases.

This passage establishes a key rule in Hanafi law: for hudud punishments (other than zina) and for qisas (retaliation), testimony requires two male witnesses; women’s testimony is not accepted. The author provides multiple proofs: a previous mursal hadith, an athar from Ali ibn Abi Talib, and a rational argument based on women’s forgetfulness (derived from Quran 2:282) and the principle that hudud are dropped by doubts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is testifying in hudud (other than zina) and qisas. The ruling is that only two male witnesses are accepted; women's testimony is rejected.
2
Locate the first proof
The author cites a mursal hadith (already mentioned) and an athar from Ali ibn Abi Talib that explicitly prohibits women's testimony in hudud and 'blood' (qisas) cases.
The athar is from Mussannaf Abd al-Razzaq.
3
Analyze the rational proof
The author links women's forgetfulness (from Quran 2:282) and the principle that hudud are waived by doubts. Since women may forget, their testimony introduces doubt, so it is not accepted in hudud and qisas.
Qisas is analogized to hudud because of similar gravity.
4
Note the exclusion of zina
Zina requires four male witnesses, not two, and women are also excluded. This passage focuses on hudud other than zina and qisas.
Zina's requirement is covered elsewhere.
5
Consider cross-madhhab differences
The Hanafi position is strict. Some other schools may accept women's testimony in certain hudud (e.g., theft), but the Hanafi school adheres to the two-male requirement.
This difference is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

[۲۸۱۴] (۵) ان سے شہادت ہے باقی حدود کی اور قصاص کی کہ ان میں دو مردوں کی گواہی قبول کی جاتی ہے اور ان میں عورتوں کی گواہی قبول نہیں کی جاتی۔ از نا میں تو چار مردوں کی گواہی چاہئے۔ ان میں عورتوں کی گواہی مقبول نہیں ہے۔ اور باقی حدود اور قصاص میں بھی عورتوں کی گواہی قبول نہیں کی جائے گی۔ صرف مردوں کی گواہی قبول کی جائے گی۔ حدود کے بارے میں او پر حدیث مرسل گزر چکی۔ قصاص بھی اسی درجے کا ہے اس لئے قصاص میں بھی عورت کی گواہی مقبول نہیں ہے (۲) اس اثر میں بھی اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ ان علی بن ابی طالب قال لا تجوز شهادة النساء في الطلاق والنكاح والحدود والدماء (الف) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب هل تجوز شهادة النساء مع الرجال في الحدود وغيره ؟ ، ج ثامن ، ص ۱۳۲۹، نمبر ۱۵۴۰۵/مصنف ابن الي هيتة ١٠٩ ء في شهادة النساء في الحدود، ج خامس ص ۵۲۸ نمبر ۲۸۷۱۱) اس اثر میں دم سے مراد قصاص ہے۔ اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ قصاص میں بھی عورتوں کی گواہی مقبول نہیں ہے (۳) آیت میں عورت کے بارے میں بتایا کہ ایک دوسرے کو یاد دلائے جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ عورتوں میں نسیان ہے۔ اور حدود اور قصاص شبہ سے بھی ساقط ہو جاتے ہیں۔ اس لئے بھی عورت کی گواہی حدود اور قصاص میں مقبول نہیں ہے۔
And from these is testimony for the remaining hudud and qisas, that in them the testimony of two men is accepted, and the testimony of women is not accepted. In zina, the testimony of four men is required. In them, women's testimony is not accepted. And in the remaining hudud and qisas, women's testimony will also not be accepted. Only men's testimony will be accepted. Regarding hudud, the mursal hadith has passed above. Qisas is of the same level, therefore women's testimony is also not accepted in qisas. Also, proof is found in this athar: 'Ali ibn Abi Talib said: Women's testimony is not permissible in divorce, marriage, hudud, and blood (cases).' (Mussannaf Abd al-Razzaq, etc.) In this athar, 'blood' refers to qisas. From this it is understood that women's testimony is also not accepted in qisas. Also, in the verse (2:282) it is mentioned that one woman reminds the other, which indicates that women are forgetful. And hudud and qisas are also dropped due to doubts. Therefore, women's testimony is not accepted in hudud and qisas.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof for testimony in hudud other than zina and in qisas. The rule is that two male witnesses are required, and women's testimony is not accepted. The proof includes a mursal hadith already mentioned and an athar from Ali ibn Abi Talib explicitly stating that women's testimony is not permissible in hudud and blood (qisas) cases. A rational proof is also given: women are considered forgetful (based on the Quranic verse about one woman reminding the other in financial matters), and hudud and qisas are waived by doubts, so women's testimony is not accepted due to the potential for doubt. The commentary applies the same rule to qisas by analogy with hudud.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation in kind for murder or injury; falls under the category of hudud for testimony purposes.
حدود (hudud): Fixed punishments for crimes against Allah (e.g., theft, zina, drinking alcohol).

Key Takeaway
In hudud and qisas, the Hanafi school requires two male witnesses and excludes women's testimony based on explicit proofs and rational reasoning.
